    Discovery and Characterization of the Potent and Selective P2X4 Inhibitor N-[4-(3-Chlorophenoxy)-3-sulfamoylphenyl]-2-phenylacetamide (BAY-1797) and Structure-Guided Amelioration of Its CYP3A4 Induction Profile

    Abstract

    The P2X4 receptor is a ligand-gated ion channel that is expressed on a variety of cell types, especially those involved in inflammatory and immune processes. High-throughput screening led to a new class of P2X4 inhibitors with substantial CYP 3A4 induction in human hepatocytes. A structure-guided optimization with respect to decreased pregnane X receptor (PXR) binding was started. It was found that the introduction of larger and more polar substituents on the ether linker led to less PXR binding while maintaining the P2X4 inhibitory potency. This translated into significantly reduced CYP 3A4 induction for compounds 71 and 73. Unfortunately, the in vivo pharmacokinetic (PK) profiles of these compounds were insufficient for the desired profile in humans. However, BAY-1797 (10) was identified and characterized as a potent and selective P2X4 antagonist. This compound is suitable for in vivo studies in rodents, and the anti-inflammatory and anti-nociceptive effects of BAY-1797 were demonstrated in a mouse complete Freund’s adjuvant (CFA) inflammatory pain model.

    Introduction

    ARTICLE SECTIONS
    Jump To
    Adenosine triphosphate (ATP) is widely recognized as an important neurotransmitter implicated in various physiological and pathophysiological roles by acting through different subtypes of purinergic receptors.(1−4) To date, seven members of the P2X family have been cloned, comprising P2X1-7.(4,5) The P2X4 receptor is a ligand-gated ion channel that is expressed on a variety of cell types, largely those known to be involved in inflammatory and immune processes, specifically including monocytes, macrophages, mast cells, and microglia.(6,7) Activation of P2X4 by extracellular ATP is known to release proinflammatory cytokines and prostaglandins such as prostaglandin E2 (PGE2).(8−10) There is growing evidence indicating the potential of P2X4 as an attractive therapeutic target in multiple pathologies, especially in chronic inflammation and neuropathic pain. Numerous lines of evidence in the literature using animal models implicate a key role of the P2X4 receptor in nociception and pain. Mice lacking the P2X4 receptor do not develop pain hypersensitivity in response to numerous inflammatory challenges such as Complete Freund’s Adjuvant (CFA), carrageenan or formalin.(8,11) In addition, mice lacking the P2X4 receptor do not develop mechanical allodynia after peripheral nerve injury, indicating an important role of P2X4 also in neuropathic pain conditions.(12,8) It has been described that P2X4 expression on microglial cells is upregulated in chronic pain conditions and is a central player in mechanisms of neuropathic and inflammatory pain.(13−15) Besides the prominent role of P2X4 in acute and chronic pain-related diseases, P2X4 is considered as a critically important mediator of neuroinflammatory and neurodegenerative diseases, respiratory diseases (e.g., asthma), and lung diseases, including fibrosis, bone metabolism, cardiovascular diseases, immunomodulation in transplantation, and atherosclerosis.(14,16−21)
    The first P2X4 inhibitor 5-BDBD (1) was described in 2004 by Bayer(22) and has been used as in vitro probe since then (see Figure 1). Until now, most reported inhibitors(23) show activity only in the high nanomolar or even micromolar range and even fewer have been described to be suitable for in vivo studies in rodents. For example, it has been shown that the antidepressive paroxetine (2)(24) acts as P2X4 inhibitor in the micromolar range in humans and rats. Müller et al. published several human selective P2X4 inhibitors like PSB-12054(25) (3) and the structurally related urea 4.(26) Another submicromolar urea BX-430 (5) was reported by Séguéla et al.(27) More potent P2X4 inhibitors like 6, 7, or 8 (NP-1815-PX) have been published by Nippon Chemiphar.(28) In 2016, Nippon Chemiphar announced the start of Phase 1 clinical trials for the treatment of neuropathic pain for their P2X4 antagonist NC-2600 (structure unpublished) together with Kyushu University.(29) Structurally related compounds like tetrazole 9 have also been disclosed by Sunovion.(30)
    Here, we report the identification and optimization of a novel class of P2X4 antagonists. This structural class shows activity on P2X4 across species, which led to the identification of BAY-1797 (compound 10) that will be discussed in detail.

    Results and Discussion

    ARTICLE SECTIONS
    Jump To
    The pharmacological hypothesis that P2X4 plays a crucial role in inflammation, sensory nerve sensitization, and proliferation (in-house and published data; for reviews, see refs (9,14)) motivated us to screen the Bayer compound collection with the aim to identify a small-molecule inhibitor of the P2X4 ion channel. Out of the 800 000 compounds that were tested in a primary high-throughput screening (HTS, Ca-flux fluorescence Imaging Plate Reader (FLIPR) assay), we identified 183 confirmed hits. After careful evaluation, two hit clusters of P2X4 antagonists were chosen for further optimization. One cluster with the most prominent member 5-BDBD (1) was already published a few years ago.(22) The current report focuses on the second cluster with compound 10 as a representative starting point for our optimization program. In this program, we used a P2X4 screening assay, evaluating the ability to inhibit ATP-induced calcium influx in human embryonic kidney (HEK) cells that were stably transfected with the human P2X4 receptor. Sulfonamide 10 is a structurally novel antagonist which is 17 times more potent (IC50 = 211 nM) than 5-BDBD (IC50 = 3710 nM) in the above-mentioned HEK cell-based human P2X4 assay (Figure 2).(31)
    Although the overall technical profile was already a good starting point with only moderate in vitro blood clearance (Clblood) of 1.88 L/(h kg) in rat hepatocytes (Table 1), this compound showed CYP3A4 induction in human hepatocytes. However, hepatocytes are not suitable for high-throughput assays to guide structure-based optimization. The primary mechanism of cytochrome P450 induction is due to increased gene transcription, which typically occurs through nuclear receptor activation. The most common nuclear receptor involved in the induction of drug metabolizing enzymes is the pregnane X receptor (PXR). To evaluate PXR nuclear receptor transactivation, a screening luciferase reporter gene assay in a human hepatoma cell line was included early in the medicinal chemistry optimization program.
    Table 1. Selected in Vitro Data for Compounds 10 (BAY-1797) and 16–30 (Optimization of Western Part)a
    a

    racemic.

    Chemistry

    Commercially available sulfuryl chloride 11a was treated with 1-(2,4-dimethoxyphenyl)methanamine under basic conditions to give the protected sulfonamide 12a (Scheme 1). Afterwards, aryl ether 13 was obtained by nucleophilic aromatic substitution reaction with 3-chlorophenol and cesium carbonate in dimethylformamide (DMF). Subsequently, the nitro group was reduced under mild conditions with platinum/vanadium (1%/2%) on activated carbon under hydrogen atmosphere to 14. Acylation of aniline 14 was performed using standard amide coupling conditions with 1-[Bis(dimethylamino)methylene]-1H-1,2,3-triazolo[4,5-b]pyridinium 3-oxide hexafluorophosphate (HATU) and N,N-diisopropylethylamine (DIPEA) in DMF. Final deprotection with trifluoroacetic acid in dichloromethane (DCM) yielded antagonists 10 and 16–30 in good overall yields.
    For further optimization of the so-called eastern ether moiety, protected sulfonamide 12a was reacted with the corresponding phenols or alcohols in the presence of cesium carbonate or sodium hydride, respectively, to nitro aryl ethers 31a–u (Scheme 2). After reduction and acylation, the final products 33–40, 42, 43, 45, 47, 49, and 51–62 were obtained by removing the 2,4-dimethoxy-benzyl group under acidic conditions.
    Esters 46, 48, and 50 were reduced with MeMgBr in THF to the corresponding tertiary alcohols 47, 49, and 51. Deprotection with trifluoroacetic acid had to be performed at the ester stage because elimination was observed when we tried to deprotect the tertiary alcohols as final step.
    Some of the alkyl and benzyl ethers were synthesized via alkylation of the phenol (Scheme 3). 2-Hydroxy-5-nitrobenzenesulfonamide 63 was reduced under acidic conditions with Pd/C under hydrogen atmosphere. The aniline 64 was subsequently acylated with the different phenylacetic acids to yield phenols 65a–d. Final alkylation was performed at 0 °C by addition of sodium hydride and the corresponding benzyl- or alkylbromide in DMF yielding sulfonamides 66–74.

    Optimization and Structure–Activity Relationships (I)

    We started to explore the structure–activity relationship (SAR) towards P2X4 inhibition at the so-called western part of the molecule by applying differently substituted phenylacetic acids, as summarized in Table 1. From an initial methyl scan, we got the first hint that substitution in the ortho-position (16) leads to a very significant boost in activity (IC50 = 10 nM) while substitution in the para-position is particularly not very well tolerated (18, IC50 = 317 nM). Introduction of further ortho-substituents such as fluorine (19, IC50 = 76 nM), chlorine (20, IC50 = 92 nM), trifluoromethyl (21, IC50 = 24 nM), or methoxy (22) all improved the human P2X4 IC50, with 22 being the most potent compound in this series (IC50 = 2 nM). However, the in vitro clearance in rat hepatocytes was significantly increased for these potent ortho-substituted compounds (MeO, Me, F with Clblood = 3.42, 2.82, 2.89 L/(h kg), respectively) and as trifluoromethyl added too much lipophilicity, chlorine was selected as the ortho-substituent with the overall best profile (log D(21) = 3.3 vs log D(20) = 3.0).
    Additional halogen substitution in the remaining ortho-position as in the cases of 23 (IC50 = 9 nM) and 24 (IC50 = 15 nM) improved potency even by factors of 10 and 6, respectively, but resulted also in compounds exhibiting higher clearance in most cases. In addition, the extra halogen burden decreased the aqueous solubility significantly (aqueous solubility of 7 mg/L for compound 20 versus 0.7 mg/L for compound 23).
    Addition of a methyl group in the benzylic position (25, IC50 = 1242 nM) clearly decreased human P2X4 potency, although in combination with chlorine in the ortho-position (26) still led to an IC50 of 134 nM. Cyclopropyl in the same position even led to a compound with no measurable activity (27, IC50 > 25 μM).
    With the improved potency and our better SAR understanding, we now turned our focus on the CYP 3A4 induction potential. With exception of fluorine derivative 19, all synthesized compounds so far exhibited a low minimum efficacious concentration (MEC) of 0.1–1.7 μM in the PXR transactivation assay (Table 1). Based on our HTS hit 10, a good correlation between PXR assay and human hepatocytes was anticipated. Therefore, low MEC values in the PXR assay would most likely translate into a significant risk for CYP3A4 induction in human hepatocytes.

    PXR Co-Crystal Structure and Docking

    To understand the molecular basis of the recognition of our molecules by the PXR protein and to guide the optimization, several compounds were selected for a co-crystallization with the PXR protein. Candidates for co-crystallization were prioritized using the thermal shift assay (TSA). For compounds that stabilized the PXR by a shift in the melting temperature ΔTm of larger than 4 K, co-crystallization experiments were initiated. This resulted in a PXR co-crystal structure with the 2-phenylpropionic acid derivative 25 (Figure 3). The compound binds to PXR with the methylbenzyl moiety inserting in a hydrophobic subpocket forming a π–π stacking interaction with Trp299 and T-stacking interactions with Phe288 and Tyr306. The sulfonamide group is coordinated via three H bonds to the side chains of Ser247, Gln285, and His407.
    Constraint docking of HTS hit 10 and of para-methyl derivative 18 using the X-ray structure of compound 25 as guidance revealed a good fit in the PXR pocket explaining the low MEC values in the PXR assay. Figure 4A shows that the phenylacetic acid substituent forms a π–π stacking with Trp299 and a T stacking with Phe288. We figured out that a larger para-substituent should disturb the stacking due to a steric clash with Tyr306 leading overall to the desired looser fit in the PXR pocket, which results in a higher MEC value. We therefore also docked the series of para-fluorine (28), para-chlorine (29), and para-trifluoromethyl (30) derivatives (Figure 4A). Subsequent testing of these compounds in the PXR assay supported this hypothesis. With increasing size of the para-substituent, these compounds showed weaker PXR binding translating into increasing MEC values (Table 1). Figure 4A shows that especially 30 cannot be accommodated with the same binding mode as 10 and 18 anymore, leading to the observed increased MEC value in the PXR assay. Unfortunately, such large substituents were not tolerated by the P2X4 SAR, leading to loss of activity as, for example, for compound 30 (IC50 = 1718 nM).

    Optimization and Structure–Activity Relationships (II)

    Altogether, our understanding of PXR docking based on the PXR-co-crystal structure of 25 was in line with our experimental findings but did not fulfill the SAR requirements for human P2X4 inhibition. Consequently, we directed our attention on the eastern SAR while keeping the ortho-chlorine substituent (compound 20) constant. We started by variations with different phenols that were prepared in nucleophilic aromatic substitution reactions, as summarized in Table 2.
    Table 2. Selected in Vitro Data for Compounds 33–40, 42, 43, 45, 47, 49, 51–58, and 66–71 (Optimization of Eastern Part)
    Unsubstituted phenol (33) as well as small substituents in ortho- (34) or meta-position (36) led to very good human P2X4 potencies with IC50 values of 10, 13, and 8 nM. Comparing the pair of 34 (IC50 = 13 nM) and 35 (IC50 = 1250 nM) demonstrated again the highly beneficial effect of the ortho-substituent of the phenylacetic acid on potency. However, PXR binding was maintained, which was in good accordance with the PXR co-crystal structure of 25.
    Having a closer look at the PXR co-crystal structure of 25, we postulated that a larger and especially more polar substituent in meta- or para-position of the eastern aryl should weaken PXR binding and by that improving the CYP induction profile. Therefore, sulfonamides 37–40, 42, 43, and 45 were synthesized and while the first four (37–40) showed higher MEC values (range: 8.4–11 μM) (with human P2X4 IC50’s of 30, 75, 67, and 93 nM, respectively), the latter and slightly larger ones (42, 43, and 45) showed no transactivation of PXR up to the highest tested concentration (MEC values > 50 μM), well in line with our hypothesis. Among these three compounds, the morpholinylamide 43 showed a very promising human P2X4 IC50 of 45 nM but later had to be discontinued due to increased clearance in rat hepatocytes (Clblood = 2.25 L/(h kg)). On first glance, compounds 37 and 39 also looked promising regardless of the still only moderate MEC for PXR binding. But despite low in vitro clearance in rat hepatocytes, 37 showed medium in vitro clearance in human liver microsomes (0.62 L/(h kg)) and a low aqueous solubility of only around 1 mg/L. The combination of these two parameters led to the deprioritization of this compound. Triazole 39, on the other hand, had an efflux ratio in the Caco-2 assay of 70 and therefore was no alternative.
    A hydroxyisopropyl scan further supported our PXR-binding hypothesis. While ortho and meta derivatives 47 (IC50 = 242 nM) and 49 (IC50 = 474 nM) showed PXR binding, the para-substituted derivative 51 (IC50 = 376 nM) showed a significantly weaker transactivation of PXR (MEC = 33 μM), matching well with the docking study as highlighted in Figure 4B–D.
    To further expand the SAR understanding regarding human P2X4 inhibition, the right aryl was then replaced by various heterocycles. Among those heterocycles, especially 5-chloro-3-pyridyl (54, IC50 = 27 nM) and 1-methyl-4-pyrazolyl (56, IC50 = 185 nM) were potent, but most likely due to their small size, they still showed significant PXR binding.
    Not surprisingly, cyclohexyl ether 57 also strongly transactivated PXR, but had a human P2X4 IC50 of 39 nM, which shows that also nonaromatic substituents were well tolerated.
    In parallel, we decided to increase the size of the right substituent and synthesized benzyl derivative 66. While 66 was extremely potent with an IC50 of 2 nM, significant PXR transactivation was maintained. In addition, this compound showed a very low aqueous solubility. Switching to 2-pyridylmethyl (67, IC50 = 26 nM) and phenethyl (68, IC50 = 19 nM) slightly decreased potency but did not improve PXR interaction.
    Encouraged by the above-described findings that aliphatic as well as larger substituents are well tolerated, and based on our increasing understanding of the PXR binding, we synthesized a set of elongated aliphatic ethers, from a small cyclobutylmethyl (69, IC50 = 5 nM) over 2-oxetanylmethyl (70, IC50 = 784 nM) towards larger and especially more polar 2-dioxanylmethyl (58, IC50 = 258 nM) and 4-tetrahydropyranylmethyl (71, IC50 = 139 nM). While the smaller and more lipophilic substituents in 57 and 69 transactivated PXR, oxetane derivative 70 showed a reduced effect (MEC = 11.4 μM), and the larger and more polar variants 58 and 71 did not significantly transactivate PXR (MEC > 25 μM). With the tetrahydropyrane derivative 71, we had finally arrived at an inhibitor with an overall improved profile, combining potency against human P2X4 (IC50 just above 100 nM), a higher MEC value of 35.6 μM, and low clearance in rat hepatocytes (Clblood 0.95 L/(h kg)). We therefore selected this compound as the starting point for the next round of optimization and went back to modify the left 2-chlorophenyl acetic acid moiety, as shown in Table 3.
    Table 3. Selected in Vitro Data for Compounds 59–62 and 72–74 (Fine-Tuning of Western Part)

    First, the chlorine was replaced again by trifluoromethyl (59, IC50 = 88 nM), difluoromethyl (60, IC50 = 111 nM), and fluorine (61, IC50 = 597 nM). While compound 59 resulted in reduced PXR transactivation, the ortho-fluorine led to a significant decrease in potency, leaving only difluoromethyl derivative 60 as an interesting candidate. Next, disubstituted phenylacetic acids were synthesized, maintaining the ortho-chlorine. Among these, compounds 72–74 showed potencies below 100 nM, with the 3-fluoro-6-chloro derivative 73 having the best overall technical profile as it showed significantly reduced PXR transactivation with an MEC value of 33 μM and displayed low in vitro clearance in rat hepatocytes (0.53 L/(h kg)). Consequently, we chose compound 73 for further profiling together with the original HTS hit 10 and the first candidate 71.

    Technical Profile of Key Compounds 10, 71, and 73

    During the optimization process starting from HTS hit 10, the potency against human P2X4 could be improved from 211 nM to IC50 = 139 and 84 nM for candidates 71 and 73, respectively. The physicochemical and pharmacokinetic parameters of these compounds are summarized in Table 4. While log D could be reduced from 2.8 to 2.2 during this process, total polar surface area (TPSA) and solubility slightly increased. Most importantly, due to the better understanding of the PXR binding based on the co-crystal structure with 25, the new candidates 71 and 73 had MEC values in the high micromolar range, indicating an improved CYP 3A4 induction profile, which was confirmed in human hepatocytes. While hit 10 showed an onset of CYP3A4 induction above 41 μg/L, this increased significantly to >10 000 and 3333 μg/L for compounds 71 and 73, respectively (Table 4).

    In summary, the improved CYP interaction profile was obtained by introduction of more polar substituents on the eastern part of the molecules. However, we observed a negative impact on the apparent permeability (53 and 43 nm/s for 71 and 73, respectively, vs 132 nm/s for hit 10) and also a slight increase in efflux (efflux ratio in the Caco-2 permeability assay of 4.3 and 5.5 for 71 and 73, respectively) compared to 10 (efflux ratio of 0.6).
    In the next step, the in vivo pharmacokinetic properties of these three compounds were evaluated by a single-dose i.v. and p.o. administration in rats. Unfortunately, candidates 71 (Clblood = 2.70 L/(h kg); t1/2 = 0.37 h, F = 21%) and 73 (Clblood = 1.31 L/(h kg); t1/2 = 0.77 h, F = 44%) displayed higher clearance, shorter half-life times, and lower or equal bioavailability compared to the starting point 10 (Clblood = 0.48 L/(h kg); t1/2 = 2.64 h, F = 43%). It appeared that despite an improved in vitro metabolic stability in rat hepatocytes, the increased efflux ratio and lower permeability led to an overall suboptimal in vivo PK profile for the anticipated once daily/twice daily dosing regimen especially due to the significantly lower half-life times for compounds 71 and 73. We therefore discontinued derivatives 71 and 73 and further profiled the HTS hit 10 in depth as it displayed the best overall pharmacokinetic (PK) profile. The backup compound 60 also showed a comparable efflux ratio of 4.5 and was therefore discontinued as well.
    Compound 10 (BAY-1797) was profiled in further P2X4 assays. It was equipotent over various species in calcium influx based assays in overexpressing 1321N1 cells (human P2X4 IC50 = 108 nM, mouse P2X4 IC50 = 112 nM, and rat P2X4 IC50 = 233 nM), as shown in Figure 5. IC50 was confirmed in patch-clamp experiments on human P2X4 in 1321N1 cells (320 nM, QPatch) (Figure 6).
    Sulfonamide 10 is a very selective P2X4 antagonist with no or very weak activity on the other P2X ion channels (human P2X1 IC50 > 50 μM; human P2X23 IC50 > 30 μM; human P2X3 IC50 = 8.3 μM; and human P2X7 IC50 = 10.6 μM). No measurable activity was seen on hERG and carbonic anhydrase II (both IC50 > 10 μM). Compound 10 was also tested against a panel of off-targets, including G-protein coupled receptors (GPCRs), ion channels, kinases, and transporters at 10 μM. An inhibitory activity against the dopamine transporter (DAT, IC50 2.17 μM) was revealed as the only hit.
    The compound has an acceptable CYP inhibition profile with IC50’s against CYP3A4, CYP2D6, CYP2C9, CYP2C8, and CYP 1A2 of >20, 4.9, 4.6, 1.8, and >20 μM, respectively.
    Compound 10 shows a slight degradation in human plasma (6% degradation after 4 h) and is stable in rat plasma as well as in aqueous buffered media between pH 1 and 10. Ames, micronucleus test (MNT), and Cytotox tests were negative.

    Antinociceptive and Anti-Inflammatory Effects of Compound 10 in a Model of Inflammatory Pain

    Inflammatory pain can be induced in rodents by intraplantar injection of Complete Freund’s Adjuvant (CFA). This model is well characterized in the literature and is routinely used for assessing antinociceptive and anti-inflammatory effects of new compounds.(32) In this inflammatory pain model, P2X4 expression in the spinal cord is described to be upregulated and P2X4 knock-out (KO) mice exhibited decreased pain hypersensitivity to mechanical stimuli.(12,33) These observations support the use of the mouse CFA inflammatory pain model to assess the in vivo efficacy of P2X4 antagonists. In a first study, the anti-inflammatory effect of compound 10 was evaluated by measuring intraplantar levels of PGE2. 10 was administered once per os (p.o.) at doses of 12.5, 25, and 50 mg/kg. Treatment with CFA showed a significant induction of PGE2 levels in the inflamed paw in comparison to the contralateral paw (mean ± standard deviation (SD); contraleral paw, 298 ± 167 mg/mL; CFA, 955 ± 340 mg/mL; Figure 7A). At the two highest tested doses (25 and 50 mg/kg), compound 10 significantly reduced intraplantar PGE2 levels (mean ± SD; 12.5 mg/kg, 890 ± 458 mg/mL; 25 mg/kg, 554 ± 289 mg/mL; 50 mg/kg, 337 ± 74 mg/mL; Figure 7A). Plasma levels of 10 one hour after dosing (Cub; mean ± SD; 12.5 mg/kg, 186 ± 43 nM; 25 mg/kg, 374 ± 70 nM; 50 mg/kg, 672 ± 154 nM) indicated that unbound plasma levels should exceed the mouse IC80 (mouse IC80 = 490 nM in 1321N1 cells) to observe a significant efficacy on PGE2 levels. The observed efficacy was then comparable to the selective cyclooxygenase-2 (COX-2) inhibitor rofecoxib (mean ± SD; rofecoxib, 327 ± 76 mg/mL; Figure 7A).
    Changes in pain-related spontaneous behavior were evaluated in the mouse CFA model over 48 h following multiple p.o. administrations of compound 10 (50 mg/kg, once daily). As previously described, we evaluated nonevoked inflammatory hyperalgesia using the dynamic weight bearing (DWB) system.(32,34−36) DWB quantifies whether a relief posture is taken by animals to avoid pain. We observed that treatment with CFA induced a significant reduction of the ipsilateral paw load 24 and 48 h after CFA injection compared to baseline values (mean ± SD; baseline, 94 ± 19%; 24h, 38 ± 23%; 48h, 49 ± 22%; Figure 7B). Daily treatment with 10 or rofecoxib significantly reduced nonevoked pain-related behavior by increasing paw load on the ipsilateral paw. While the effect of 10 was not significant and inferior to the effect of rofecoxib at 24 h (mean ± SD; compound 10, 63 ± 11%; rofecoxib, 90 ± 32%; Figure 7B), a comparable efficacy was observed at 48 h (mean ± SD; compound 10, 80 ± 32%; rofecoxib, 87 ± 37%; Figure 7B). Relevant unbound plasma levels of compound 10 were measured 80 min after dosing (Cub; mean ± SD; 50 mg/kg, 548 ± 32 nM).

    Conclusions

    ARTICLE SECTIONS
    Jump To
    In summary, we have identified and characterized 10 (BAY-1797) as potent and selective P2X4 inhibitor. Anti-inflammatory and antinociceptive effects of 10 were demonstrated in mouse CFA inflammatory pain models.
    BAY-1797 together with the negative control BAY-207 (compound 27) are available at the Structure Genomics Center (SGC) within their chemical probe program.(37) Chemical probes are chemical biology tool compounds, where a selective small-molecule modulator of a protein’s function allows answering research questions about a specific target and hence improving the understanding of the physiological or pathological role of a target.(38)
    Due to its high risk for CYP 3A4 induction in the clinic, compound 10 was not considered for further profiling toward clinical testing. In the course of the optimization program, we performed a structure-guided optimization to abolish PXR binding by introduction of longer and more polar substituents on the ether linker and larger groups in para-position on the phenylacetic acid group. This translated into significantly improved CYP induction profiles for tetrahydropyranmethyl ethers 71 and 73. Unfortunately, the in vivo PK profiles of these compounds were insufficient for the desired profile of once or twice daily oral dosing in humans and these compounds had to be terminated. Further optimization work focused on maintaining this favorable CYP induction profile and combining it with further improved PK and potency profiles will be reported in due course.

    Experimental Section

    ARTICLE SECTIONS
    Jump To

    Chemistry

    General

    All reagents and solvents were used as purchased, unless otherwise specified. Intermediates 11a, 11b, and 63 were purchased, and all others were prepared as described below. All air- and moisture-sensitive reactions were carried out in oven-dried (at 120 °C) glassware under an inert atmosphere of argon. The purity of most final compounds was ≥ 95%, determined by liquid chromatography mass spectrometry (LC–MS) and 1H NMR. Reactions were monitored by thin-layer chromatography (TLC) and ultraperformance liquid chromatography (UPLC) analysis with a Waters Acquity UPLC MS Single Quad system; column: Acquity UPLC BEH C18 1.7 μm, 50 × 2.1 mm; eluent A: water (H2O) + 0.2 vol % aqueous (aq) ammonia (NH3, 32%) and eluent B: acetonitrile (MeCN); gradient: 0–1.6 min 1–99% B, 1.6–2.0 min 99% B; flow: 0.8 mL/min; temperature: 60 °C; diode array detector (DAD) scan: 210–400 nm. Flash chromatography was carried out using a Biotage Isolera One system with a 200–400 nm variable detector, using Biotage SNAP KP-Sil or KP-NH cartridges. Preparative high-performance liquid chromatography (HPLC) was carried out with a Waters AutoPurification MS Single Quad system; column: Waters XBridge C18 5 μm, 100 mm × 30 mm; eluent A: H2O + 0.2 vol % aq NH3 (32%) and eluent B: MeCN; gradient: 0–5.5 min 5–100% B; flow: 70 mL/min; temperature: 25 °C; DAD scan: 210–400 nm. Analytical TLC was carried out on aluminum-backed plates coated with Merck Kieselgel 60 F254, with visualization under UV light at 254 nm. All NMR spectra were recorded on Bruker Avance III HD spectrometers. 1H NMR spectra were obtained at 300, 400, 500, or 600 MHz and referenced to the residual solvent signal (2.49 ppm for DMSO-d6).
    13C NMR spectra were obtained at 101 or 151 MHz and also referenced to the residual solvent signal (39.7 ppm for DMSO-d6). All spectra were obtained at ambient temperature (22 ± 1 °C). 1H NMR data are reported as follows: chemical shift (δ) in ppm, multiplicity (s = singlet, d = doublet, t = triplet, q = quartet, br = broad, m = multiplet), and integration. Mass spectra were recorded on LC-MS instruments. Analytical LC-MS and UPLC-MS conditions are reported in the Supporting Information.

    General Procedure for Nucleophilic Aromatic Substitution

    GP1.1
    Sulfonamide 12 (1.29 mmol) was dissolved in acetonitrile (10 mL) and cesium carbonate (1.29 mmol, 1.0 eq), and the corresponding alcohols (1.29 mmol, 1.0 eq) were added. Stirring was continued at 85–100 °C until TLC showed consumption of starting material. The solvent was removed under reduced pressure, followed by addition of water and dichloromethane. Afterward, the phases were separated, the organic phase was dried, and concentrated in vacuo. The crude product was either used without further purification or purified as indicated in the examples.
    GP1.2
    Sulfonamide 12 (1.29 mmol) was dissolved in dimethylformamide (20 mL) and the corresponding alcohol (1.94 mmol, 1.5 eq) was added followed by the addition of sodium hydride (9.05 mmol, 1.5 eq). Stirring was continued for aliphatic alcohols at room temperature and for phenols at 110 °C until TLC showed consumption of starting material. The reaction mixture was cooled to 0 °C and water and ethyl acetate was carefully added. Afterward, the phases were separated and the aqueous phase was extracted three times with ethyl acetate. The combined organic phases were dried and concentrated in vacuo. The crude was either used without further purification or purified as indicated in the examples.

    General Procedure for Reduction of Nitro Compounds

    GP2.1
    Nitro compound 13, 31, or 63 (0.85 mmol) was dissolved in tetrahydrofuran (25 mL) and Pd/C (0.09 mmol, 0.1 eq) was added. The flask was evacuated three times and flushed with hydrogen (1 bar) and stirring was continued at room temperature. After completion of the reaction, the mixture was filtered and concentrated in vacuo. The crude was used without further purification.
    GP2.2
    Nitro compound 13, 31a–u, or 63 (1.29 mmol) was dissolved in dioxane (6 mL) and tinn(II)chloride dihydrate (6.46 mmol, 5.0 eq) was added, and the reaction mixture was stirred for 2 h at 70 °C. After cooling to room temperature, the reaction mixture was filtered and concentrated in vacuo. The filtrate was either used without further purification or purified as indicated in the examples.

    General Procedure for Acylation of Anilines

    GP3.1
    Aniline 14, 32a–u, or 64 (0.17 mmol) was dissolved in dimethylformamide (5 mL) followed by the addition of the corresponding acid chloride (0.6 mmol), potassium carbonate (0.5 mmol). The reaction mixture was stirred at room at 100 °C until TLC showed consumption of starting material. After cooling to r.t., dichloromethane and water were added to the reaction mixture and phases were separated. The aqueous phase was extracted three times with ethyl acetate, the combined organic phase was dried, and the solvent was removed under reduced pressure. The crude was used without further purification.
    GP3.2
    Aniline 14, 32, or 64 (1.29 mmol) was dissolved in dimethylformamide (6 mL) followed by the addition of the corresponding acid (1.42 mmol, 1.1 eq), N,N-diisopropylethylamine (6.46 mmol, 5.0 eq), and HATU (2.07 mmol, 1.6 eq). The reaction mixture was either stirred overnight at room temperature or heated at 50 °C until TLC showed consumption of starting material. After cooling to room temperature, the reaction mixture was concentrated in vacuo. Ethyl acetate and water were added, and the organic phase was dried and concentrated in vacuo. The crude was used without further purification.
    GP3.3
    Aniline 14, 32, or 64 (0.25 mmol), the corresponding acid (0.50 mmol, 2.0 eq), HATU (0.50 mmol, 2.0 eq), and N-methylmorpholine (1.0 mmol, 2.0 eq) were dissolved in NMP (2.83 mL, containing 2.5% DMAP) and stirred for 2 h at room temperature, followed by stirring overnight at 60 °C. The reaction mixture was concentrated in vacuo, and the crude was used without further purification.

    General Procedure for Deprotection of 2,4-Dimethoxybenzyl Sulfonamides

    GP4
    Crude amide (1.29 mmol) was dissolved in dichloromethane (5–10 mL), trifluoroacetic acid (64.5 mmol, 50 eq) was added, and the reaction mixture was stirred at room temperature until TLC showed consumption of starting material. The reaction mixture was concentrated in vacuo, ethyl acetate and water were added to the crude, the organic phase was dried, and the solvent was removed under reduced pressure. The resulting residue was purified as indicated in the examples. Purification without aqueous extraction was also possible but made the HPLC purification more difficult.

    General Procedue Alkylation of Phenols

    GP5
    Phenol 65 (0.20 mmol) was dissolved in dimethylformamide (3–5 mL), cooled in an ice bath, and treated with sodium hydride (55% purity, 0.24 mmol, 1.2 eq). After stirring for 20 min, the corresponding alkyl or benzyl halide (0.30 mmol, 1.5 eq) was added and the reaction mixture was allowed to warm up and stirred at room temperature (if not indicated otherwise) until TLC showed consumption of starting material. Water and ethyl acetate were added, the organic phase was washed twice with water, dried, and concentrated in vacuo. The crude was purified as indicated in the examples to yield pure final compound.

    N-[4-(3-Chlorophenoxy)-3-sulfamoylphenyl]-2-phenylacetamide (10, BAY-1797)

    15a (150 mg, 0.264 mmol) was converted according to GP4 to 10. The pure compound was obtained after preparative HPLC (Waters XBridge C18 5μ 100 mm × 30 mm, acetonitrile/water + 0.2% aqueous ammonia (32%)) (28 mg, 0.066 mmol, 25% yield, 95% purity). LC-MS (method B): Rt = 1.12 min; MS (ESIpos): m/z = 417 (M + H)+. 1H NMR (400 MHz, DMSO-d6) δ [ppm]: 3.65 (s, 2H), 6.92–7.00 (m, 1H), 7.04–7.11 (m, 2H), 7.15–7.22 (m, 1H), 7.22–7.30 (m, 1H), 7.30–7.45 (m, 7H), 7.77–7.85 (m, 1H), 8.16–8.23 (m, 1H), 10.46 (s, 1H). 13C NMR (101 MHz, DMSO-d6) δ [ppm]: 43.3, 117.0, 118.6, 118.7, 121.5, 123.3, 123.9, 126.6, 128.4, 129.1, 131.1, 133.6, 135.2, 135.3, 135.7, 147.5, 158.0, 169.3.

    2-Chloro-N-(2,4-dimethoxybenzyl)-5-nitrobenzenesulfonamide (12a)

    To a solution of 11a (10.8 g, 42.2 mmol) in dichloromethane (108 mL) were added sodium bicarbonate (7.09 g, 84.4 mmol) and 1-(2,4-dimethoxyphenyl)methanamine (7.05 g, 42.2 mmol). The mixture was stirred overnight. The reaction mixture was concentrated in vacuo, followed by addition of water (75 mL) and ethyl acetate (75 mL). After stirring for 10 min, the resulting precipitate was separated by filtration, and it was dried at 40 °C overnight in vacuo to yield 12a (14.1 g, 36.5 mmol, 86% yield). LC-MS (method A): Rt = 1.17 min; MS (ESIneg): m/z = 385 (M – H)+. 1H NMR (400 MHz, DMSO-d6) δ [ppm]: 3.56 (s, 3H), 3.61 (s, 3H), 4.08 (s, 2H), 6.10 (d, 1H), 6.26 (dd, 1H), 7.04 (d, 1H), 7.79 (d, 1H), 8.19 (d, 1H), 8.28 (dd, 1H), 8.45 (s, 1H).

    N-(2,4-Dimethoxybenzyl)-2-fluoro-5-nitrobenzenesulfonamide (12b)

    To a solution of 1-(2,4-dimethoxyphenyl)methanamine (0.669 g, 4.00 mmol) in dichloromethane (40 mL) was added under ice cooling N-ethyl-N-isopropylpropan-2-amine (1.29 g, 10.0 mmol). Over 25 min, a solution of 11b (0.958 g, 4.00 mmol) in dichloromethane (10 mL) was slowly added. Stirring was continued under ice cooling for 2 h, followed by stirring at room temperature overnight. It was washed with water, dried over sodium sulfate, and concentrated in vacuo. Column chromatography on a Biotage Isolera system (silica gel, gradient n-hexane/ethyl acetate) gave 12b (400 mg, 1.08 mmol, 27% yield, purity 70%). LC-MS (method A): Rt = 1.12 min; MS (ESIneg): m/z = 369 (M – H)+.

    2-(3-Chlorophenoxy)-N-[(2,4-dimethoxyphenyl)methyl]-5-nitrobenzene-1-sulfonamide (13)

    12a (49.9 g, 129 mmol) was dissolved in acetonitrile (500 mL) under nitrogen atmosphere. Cesium carbonate (42.0 g, 129 mmol) and 3-chlorophenol (14 mL, 130 mmol) were added successively under stirring. The reaction was heated overnight at 110 °C. After completion of the reaction, the mixture was cooled to r.t. and DCM (500 mL) was added. The suspension was filtered and the precipitate was washed with DCM. Afterward, the solid was suspended in water and filtered. The filtrate was evaporated and DCM/water (250 mL/250 mL) was added, and the precipitate was filtered off and washed with water. The combined product (13) was used without further purification in the next step (83.6 g, 84%). LC-MS (method B): Rt = 1.38 min; MS (ESIpos): m/z = 496 (M + H)+. 1H NMR (400 MHz, DMSO-d6): δ [ppm]= 3.57 (s, 3H), 3.65 (s, 3H), 4.13 (s, 2H), 6.23 (d, 1H), 6.32–6.37 (m, 1H), 6.95–7.02 (m, 1H), 7.08–7.14 (m, 2H), 7.16–7.21 (m, 1H), 7.36–7.45 (m, 1H), 7.49–7.59 (m, 1H), 8.20 (s, 1H), 8.27–8.37 (m, 2H).

    5-Amino-2-(3-chlorophenoxy)-N-(2,4-dimethoxybenzyl)benzenesulfonamide (14)

    The reaction was performed in an autoclave reactor. 13 (13.0 g, 27.1 mmol) was suspended in toluene (150 mL) and water (150 mL) was added. The pH was adjusted to 3 by addition of phosphoric acid (85%, 40 μL). Afterward, platinum/vanadium (1%/2%) on activated carbon (ca. 50% wetted powder) (3.34 g, 1% purity, 136 μmol) was added. The reaction mixture was set under hydrogen atmosphere (6.48 bar), the temperature was adjusted to 100 °C, and stirred for 4 h (caution: raise in pressure observed). After completion of the reaction, the catalyst was filtered off. The phases were separated, and the aqueous phase was extracted three times with ethyl acetate. The combined organic phases were washed with half-saturated aq. NaCl and dried over sodium sulfate. After filtration, the solvent was removed under reduced pressure to yield 14 (12.4 g, 102%). The crude was used without further purification in the next step. LC-MS (method A): Rt = 1.26 min; MS (ESIpos): m/z = 449 (M + H)+. 1H NMR (400 MHz, DMSO-d6): δ [ppm]= 3.67–3.74 (m, 6H), 3.98 (d, 2H), 5.47 (s, 2H), 6.39–6.48 (m, 2H), 6.70–6.77 (m, 1H), 6.78–6.85 (m, 2H), 6.87–6.94 (m, 1H), 7.03–7.10 (m, 2H), 7.11–7.15 (m, 1H), 7.27–7.37 (m, 1H), 7.41–7.48 (m, 1H).

    N-{4-(3-Chlorophenoxy)-3-[(2,4-dimethoxybenzyl)sulfamoyl]phenyl}-2-phenylacetamide (15a)

    N-[4-(3-Chlorophenoxy)-3-sulfamoylphenyl]-2-(2-methoxyphenyl)acetamide (22)

    Step 3.     5-Amino-2-hydroxybenzenesulfonamide Hydrochloride (64)

    To a solution of 63 (10.9 g, 50.0 mmol) in methanol (250 mL) were added under argon aqueous 2M-HCl (25 mL, 50 mmol) and 10% Pd/C (1.5 g). After stirring under an atmosphere of hydrogen for 20 h, the catalyst was removed by filtration over a PTFE membrane and the filtrate was concentrated in vacuo affording crude 64 that was used in the next steps without further purification (11.2 g, 0.499 mmol, 99% yield, 95% purity). LC-MS (method C): Rt = 0.20 min; MS (ESIneg): m/z = 187 (M – H)+. 1H NMR (400 MHz, deuterium oxide) δ [ppm]: 7.23 (d, 1H), 7.57 (dd, 1H), 7.83 (d, 1H).

    2-(2-Chlorophenyl)-N-(4-hydroxy-3-sulfamoylphenyl)acetamide (65a)

    To a suspension of 64 (2.25 g, 10.0 mmol) in tetrahydrofuran (75 mL) were added (2-chlorophenyl)acetic acid (1.88 g, 11 mmol), N,N-diisopropylethylamine (6.46 g, 50 mmol), and HATU (4.18 g, 11 mmol). The reaction mixture was stirred overnight at room temperature. Then, it was concentrated in vacuo, followed by extraction from ethyl acetate/water. The organic phase was washed with water, dried over sodium sulfate, and concentrated in vacuo. Column chromatography on a Biotage Isolera system (silica gel, gradient dichloromethane to dichloromethane/methanol 80/20) led to 65a (1.60 g, 4.70 mmol, 47% yield, 90% purity). LC-MS (method A): Rt = 0.85 min; MS (ESIpos): m/z = 341 (M + H)+. 1H NMR (400 MHz, DMSO-d6) δ [ppm]: 3.78 (s, 2H), 6.85–6.95 (m, 3H), 7.25–7.33 (m, 2H), 7.36–7.46 (m, 2H), 7.59 (dd, 1H), 7.93 (d, 1H), 10.17 (s, 1H), 10.39 (s, 1H).

    2-(2-Chloro-3-fluorophenyl)-N-(4-hydroxy-3-sulfamoylphenyl)acetamide (65b)

    To a suspension of 64 (0.450 g, 2.00 mmol) in tetrahydrofuran (20 mL) were added (2-chloro-3-fluorophenyl)acetic acid (0.415 g, 2.20 mmol), N,N-diisopropylethylamine (1.29 g, 10.0 mmol), and HATU (0.837 g, 2.20 mmol). The reaction mixture was stirred overnight at room temperature, followed by concentration in vacuo and extraction from ethyl acetate/water. The organic phase was washed with water, dried over sodium sulfate, and concentrated in vacuo. As LC-MS showed mostly bisacylated product, the residue was redissolved in tetrahydrofuran and treated for 24 h with aqueous 1 M NaOH (15 mL). After removing tetrahydrofuran in vacuo, it was neutralized with diluted hydrochloric acid, followed by extraction with ethyl acetate. The organic phase was washed with water, dried over sodium sulfate, and concentrated in vacuo. Purification by preparative HPLC (Chromatorex C-18 10 μm, 125 mm × 30 mm, acetonitrile/water + 0.1% formic acid) led to 65b (240 mg, 0.669 mmol, 33% yield, 99% purity). LC-MS (method A): Rt = 0.86 min; MS (ESIpos): m/z = 359 (M + H)+. 1H NMR (400 MHz, DMSO-d6) δ [ppm]: 3.86 (s, 2H), 6.90–7.00 (m, 3H), 7.24–7.41 (m, 3H), 7.60 (dd, 1H), 7.94 (d, 1H), 10.24 (s, 1H), 10.45 (s, 1H).

    2-(2-Chloro-5-fluorophenyl)-N-(4-hydroxy-3-sulfamoylphenyl)acetamide (65c)

    To 64 (1.24 g, 5.50 mmol) in tetrahydrofuran (55 mL) were added (2-chloro-5-fluorophenyl)acetic acid (1.14 g, 6.05 mmol) and N,N-diisopropylethylamine (4.8 mL, 28 mmol) at 0 °C. Afterward, HATU (2.30 g, 6.05 mmol) was added and the reaction mixture was stirred for 1 h at 0 °C. The reaction was allowed to come to room temperature and stirring was continued overnight. Afterward, the solvent was removed under reduced pressure and the crude was partitioned in ethyl acetate/water. The organic phase was washed with water, dried over sodium sulfate, and concentrated in vacuo. The crude was suspended in DCM, warmed up, and stirring was continued at room temperature overnight. The precipitate was filtered off and washed with DCM to yield 65c (0.35 g, 18% yield, 99% purity). LC-MS (method A): Rt = 0.89 min; MS (ESIpos): m/z = 359 (M + H)+. 1H NMR (400 MHz, DMSO-d6): δ [ppm] = 3.81 (s, 2H), 6.88–6.99 (m, 3H), 7.14–7.23 (m, 1H), 7.31 (dd, 1H), 7.47–7.52 (m, 1H), 7.60 (dd, 1H), 7.88–7.99 (m, 1H), 10.21 (s, 1H), 10.43 (s, 1H).

    2-(2-Chloro-5-fluorophenyl)-N-[3-sulfamoyl-4-(tetrahydro-2H-pyran-4-ylmethoxy)phenyl]acetamide (73)

    According to general procedure GP5, 65c (144 mg, 0.40 mmol) and 4-(bromomethyl)tetrahydro-2H-pyran (107 mg, 0.60 mmol) were converted to 73 (stirring overnight at room temperature was followed by stirring at 50 °C for 6 h), and it was purified by preparative HPLC (Chromatorex C-18 10 μm, 125 mm × 30 mm, acetonitrile/water + 0.1% formic acid) (40 mg, 0.0875 mmol, 22% yield, 95% purity). LC-MS (method A): Rt = 1.04 min; MS (ESIpos): m/z = 457 (M + H)+. 1H NMR (400 MHz, DMSO-d6) δ [ppm]: 1.29 (ddd, 2H), 1.69–1.77 (m, 2H), 2.10–2.24 (m, 1H), 3.28–3.36 (m, 2H), 3.83 (s, 2H), 3.84–3.91 (m, 2H), 3.95 (d, 2H), 6.92 (s, 2H), 7.15–7.23 (m, 2H), 7.34 (dd, 1H), 7.50 (dd, 1H), 7.76 (dd, 1H), 8.02 (d, 1H), 10.33 (s, 1H). 13C NMR (101 MHz, DMSO-d6) δ [ppm]: 29.2, 33.9, 40.5, 66.7, 73.1, 113.7, 115.4, 115.6, 118.9, 119.0, 119.2, 124.1, 129.0, 129.0, 130.5, 130.6, 131.0, 131.4, 136.1, 136.2, 151.1, 159.3, 161.7, 167.2.

    2-(2-Chloro-6-fluorophenyl)-N-[3-sulfamoyl-4-(tetrahydro-2H-pyran-4-ylmethoxy)phenyl]acetamide (74)

    According to general procedure GP5, 65d (53.8 mg, 0.15 mmol) and 4-(bromomethyl)tetrahydro-2H-pyran (40.3 mg, 0.23 mmol) were converted to 74 (stirring over the weekend at room temperature), and it was purified by preparative HPLC (Chromatorex C-18 10 μm, 125 mm × 30 mm, acetonitrile/water + 0.1% formic acid) (3 mg, 0.00657 mmol, 4% yield, 99% purity). LC-MS (method A): Rt = 1.02 min; MS (ESIpos): m/z = 457 (M + H)+. 1H NMR (400 MHz, DMSO-d6) δ [ppm]: 1.22–1.37 (m, 2H), 1.68–1.79 (m, 2H), 2.09–2.24 (m, 1H), 3.26–3.36 (m, 2H), 3.87 (s, 4H), 3.94 (d, 2H), 6.92 (s, 2H), 7.17 (d, 1H), 7.25 (ddd, 1H), 7.33–7.42 (m, 2H), 7.74 (dd, 1H), 8.02 (d, 1H), 10.41 (s, 1H).

    In Vivo Models: Animals

    Female adult C57BL/6N mice (≈20 g; Taconic) were acclimatized for 2 weeks and maintained in a 12 h light/dark cycle with access to food and water ad libitum. The animals were randomized and housed in groups of six per cage. All experiments were performed in strict compliance with company, regional, and federal guidelines for the use of laboratory animals. The experiments were approved and executed in accordance with policies and directives of LAGESO (Landesamt für Gesundheit und Soziales Berlin; Germany; A0195/13). All efforts were made to minimize suffering.

    CFA Inflammatory Pain Model

    Female adult C57BL/6N mice received intraplantar injection of Complete Freund’s Adjuvant (CFA) (30 μL, 1 mg/mL, Sigma) into the left hind paw under isoflurane anesthesia.

    Drugs and Vehicles

    Both BAY-1797 and rofecoxib were formulated in EtOH/Solutol/NaCl (1:5:94%). For dynamic weight bearing evaluation, Rofecoxib (10 mg/kg), BAY-1797 (50 mg/kg), and vehicle are administered in a volume of 5 mL/kg once daily for 2 days starting 1 day after CFA injection. Rofecoxib is administered for 3 h and BAY-1797 is given 1 h prior to behavioral testing in DWB on days 1 and 2, respectively. For the determination of intraplantar PGE2 concentrations, Rofecoxib (10 mg/kg), BAY-1797 (12.5, 25, or 50 mg/kg), and vehicle are administered in a volume of 5 mL/kg once on day 2 after CFA injection. Rofecoxib is administered for 3 h, and BAY-1797 is given 1 h prior to paw samples collection.

    Determination of PGE2 Concentrations

    As PGE2 in biologically active matrices is rapidly converted to its metabolites, very low PGE2 concentrations are detectable in tissue, urine, or blood samples. For this reason, measurement of PGE2 metabolites is advisable to provide a reliable estimate of actual PGE2 production. Paw biopsy samples taken 1 h after oral dosing with BAY-1797 or rofecoxib were used in PGE2 metabolite enzyme immunoassay (EIA) kit (Cayman Chemicals, Ann Arbor; #514531) converting 13,14-dihydro-15-keto PGA2 and 13,14-dihydro-15-keto PGE2 to a stable derivative that can be easily quantified by EIA. The detection limit of this kit is 2 pg/mL. The assay was performed according to manufacturer’s protocol.

    Dynamic Weight Bearing Evaluation

    The automated DWB device (Bioseb, Boulogne, France) consists of a Plexiglas enclosure (11 cm × 11 cm), a floor sensor composed of 44 × 44 captors detecting pressure variation, and a camera placed above the enclosure to validate the animal position during the analysis. DWB recording took place 24 and 48 h after intraplantar CFA injection and 1 h after oral administration of BAY-1797 or rofecoxib. The mice were able to move freely in the enclosure during a total time of 10 min, starting with a habituation phase of 5 min followed by a recording phase of 5 min, in which the weight distribution and the video were recorded. We analyzed the recordings during the entire time period using the DWB software (v1.4.1.28.), as previously described.(39,40) We considered a zone as valid when the following parameters were detected: ≥0.8 g on one captor with minimum of two adjacent captors recording ≥1.0 g. A time segment was considered as valid if greater than or equal to three stable pictures were detected. We validated, in a semiautomated way, for each sequence, the position of the paws according to the video recording. At the end of analysis, we extracted the mean over the time of the weight value applied by each paw on the floor.

    Human P2X4 FLIPR Assay in HEK Cells

    HEK293 cells stably expressing human P2X4 were plated in poly-d-lysine-coated 384-well plates at a seeding density of 30 000 cells/well and incubated overnight. P2X4 function was assessed by measuring intracellular calcium changes using the calcium-chelating dye Fluo8-AM (Molecular Devices) on a fluorescent imaging plate reader (FLEX/FLIPR station; Molecular Devices). On the day of the assay, the medium was removed and the cells were incubated for 30 min at 37 °C and 5% CO2 in 30 μL of dye buffer (Hank’s balanced salt solution, 10 mM 4-(2-hydroxyethyl)-1-piperazineethanesulfonic acid (HEPES), 1.8 mM CaCl2, 1 mM MgCl2, 2 mM probenecid, 5 mM d-glucose monohydrate, 5 μM Fluo8-AM, pH = 7.4). Compounds diluted in probenecid buffer (Hank’s balanced salt solution, 10 mM HEPES, 1.8 mM CaCl2, 1 mM MgCl2, 2 mM probenecid, 5 mM d-glucose monohydrate, pH = 7.4) were added in a volume of 10 μL and allowed to incubate for 30 min at room temperature. The final assay dimethyl sulfoxide (DMSO) concentration was 0.5%. The agonist, Bz-ATP (Tocris), was added in a volume of 10 μL at a concentration representing the EC80 value. The EC80 value of Bz-ATP was determined each assay day prior to compound profiling. The fluorescence was measured for an interval of 120 at 2 s intervals. The excitation and emission wavelengths used to monitor fluorescence were 470–495 and 515–575 nm, respectively. The data were analyzed based on the increase in peak relative fluorescence units (RFU) compared to the basal fluorescence and normalized to the agonist control. The compounds were tested in triplicates per plate, and mean values were plotted in Excel XLFit to determine IC50 values, percentage of maximal inhibition, and the Hill coefficients.

    Investigation of in Vitro Metabolic Stability in Rat Hepatocytes

    Hepatocytes from Han/Wistar rats were isolated via a two-step perfusion method. After perfusion, the liver was carefully removed from the rat: the liver capsule was opened and the hepatocytes were gently shaken out into a Petri dish with ice-cold Williams’ medium E (WME). The resulting cell suspension was filtered through sterile gaze in 50 mL falcon tubes and centrifuged at 50g for 3 min at room temperature. The cell pellet was resuspended in 30 mL of WME and centrifuged twice through a Percoll gradient at 100g. The hepatocytes were washed again with WME and resuspended in medium containing 5% fetal calf serum (FCS). Cell viability was determined by trypan blue exclusion. For the metabolic stability assay, liver cells were distributed in WME containing 5% FCS to glass vials at a density of 1.0 × 106 vital cells/mL. The test compound was added to a final concentration of 1 μM. During incubation, the hepatocyte suspensions were continuously shaken at 580 rpm and aliquots were taken at 2, 8, 16, 30, 45, and 90 min, to which equal volumes of cold methanol were immediately added. Samples were frozen at −20 °C overnight, subsequently centrifuged for 15 min at 3000 rpm, and the supernatant was analyzed with an Agilent 1200 HPLC-system with LC/MS-MS detection. The half-life of a test compound was determined from the concentration–time plot. From the half-life, the intrinsic clearances and the hepatic in vivo blood clearance (CL) and maximal oral bioavailability (Fmax) were calculated using the “well stirred” liver model together with the additional parameters liver blood flow, specific liver weight, and amount of liver cells in vivo and in vitro. The following parameter values were used: Liver blood flow, 4.2 L/(h kg); specific liver weight, 32 g/kg; liver cells in vivo, 1.1 × 108 cells/g liver; liver cells in vitro, 1.0 × 106/mL.

    PXR Transactivation Assay

    To evaluate PXR nuclear receptor transactivation, a screening luciferase reporter gene assay in human HepG2 cells was applied. Transiently co-transfected HepG2 cells or stably transfected DPX-2 cells (Puracyp, Carlsbad, CA) containing the human PXR gene and a luciferase reporter gene under control of the human CYP3A4 promotor were used. The cells were seeded in 384-well plates and cultivated at 37 °C in humidified air with 5% CO2. Co-transfections with the two vectors were performed using FuGENE HD transfection reagent (Promega, Madison, WI) according to the manufacturer’s instructions; 24 h after seeding, the cells were treated with test compound (2 nM–50 μM, 0.5% DMSO) prepared by serial dilution of 1:3. Rifampicin was incubated in the same manner as positive control. In addition, for the normalization of the luminescence signal, the cells were incubated with rifampicin at a concentration of 16.5 μM corresponding to 100% activation, as well as DMSO for background luminescence corresponding to 0% activation (n = 32 wells each). The cells were lyzed and incubated with the luciferase substrate ONE-Glo Reagent (Promega, Madison, WI) according to the manufacturer’s instructions, and luminescence signal was detected in a plate reader. The transient assay was validated with a standard set of inducers (e.g., bosentan, carbamazepine) and noninducers (e.g., penicilin V, fluconazole). In addition, transiently transfected HepG2 cells and stably transfected DPX-2 cells were cross-validated using a set of 30 test compounds with different PXR transactivation potentials. From the dose–response curves, a concentration-dependent increase of the luciferase activity above a certain threshold (4-fold standard deviation of the DMSO background for transiently transfected cells and >10% of the rifampicin control for DPX-2 cells) was classified as PXR transactivation.

    CYP Induction Assay

    Human hepatocytes are seeded at a density of ∼40 000 cells/96 well in a collagen sandwich and cultured for 1 day before compound treatment. The cells are treated with a 1:3 serial dilution of 8 concentrations for two consecutive days with media change every day. After 48 h of compound treatment, the cells are lysed and mRNA is prepared by the state-of-the-art magnetic beads technique. Briefly, approximately 24 h after the last treatment, hepatocytes were harvested for mRNA isolation. Thus, cell culture medium was removed from each well, cells were washed with 150 μL supplement free cell culture medium prior to cell lysis, cells were lysed with 100 μL lysis buffer containing proteinase K (50 μg/μL; final concentration in well 30 ng/μL), and final cell lysates were stored at −80 °C. mRNA was isolated using the Dynabeads mRNA Direct Kit (Life Technologies); 150 μL of each cell lysate was mixed with 100 μg magnetic beads, incubated for a few minutes, then the supernatant was removed and beads were washed twice with Washing Buffer A and twice with Washing Buffer B with 200 and 100 μL per well, respectively. Single-stranded cDNA was prepared from mRNA with the High Capacity RNA to cDNA Kit (Life Technologies). RT Master Mix (20 μL) was added to each well and transcribed for 60 min at 37 °C using the Gene Amp PCR System 9700 thermocycling program (Biometra). The RT Master Mix is composed of 10× RT buffer, 25× deoxyNTPs, 10× Random hexamers, RNase Inhibitor (20 U/μL), MultiScribe reverse transcriptase (50 U/μL), and RNase-free water. The prepared cDNA samples were stored at −80 °C prior to analysis by quantitative real-time polymerase chain reaction (qRT-PCR). Quantitative RT-PCR was carried out on a QuantStudio7 Flex PCR system (Applied Biosystems) according to manufacturer’s protocol. A primer mix was prepared for each gene expression assay. A typical primer mix contained TaqMan Fast Advanced Master Mix (1×), Gene Expression Assay (1×, 900 nM forward and reverse primers), and RNase-free water and was added to the cDNA. The relative quantity of the target cDNA compared to that of the control cDNA (Actin, Tubulin) was determined by the ΔΔCt method. Relative quantification measures the change in mRNA expression in a test sample relative to that in a control sample (e.g., vehicle-treated). In summary, CYP induction is calculated based on the ΔΔCt method and expressed as fold induction over vehicle-treated control.

    PXR Protein Production and Co-crystal Structure Determination

    An expression construct similar to the one reported by Wang et al.,(41) coding for a peptide of the Nuclear Receptor Coactivator NCOA1 (residues 678-700) fused via a Gly-Gly-Ser-Gly-Gly linker to the C-terminus of the ligand binding domain of PXR (residues 129 to 434), was expressed in Escherichia coli, purified via affinity chromatography (His-Trap, 5 mL), ion-exchange chromatography (Mono S HR 10/10), and gelfiltration (Superdex75 26/60, buffer 20 mM Tris pH 7.8, 250 mM NaCl, 2.5 mM EDTA, 5% (v/v) glycerol, 5 mM dithiothreitol (DTT)), concentrated to 12.1 mg/mL, and shock-frozen in liquid nitrogen. Compounds were selected for co-crystallization based on their stabilizing effect in the thermal shift assay (TSA; for assay details, see the Supporting Information). For co-crystallization with compound 25, PXR was thawn, supplemented with 10 mM compound (from a 200 mM stock solution in DMSO) and incubated at 277 K for 24 h. Crystals were set up using the vapor diffusion method in hanging drops made of 1 μL of protein–ligand solution and 1 μL of reservoir (100 mM imidazole pH 8.0, 17% (v/v) 2-methyl-2,4-pentanediol). Crystals were additionally soaked in 20 mM compound 25 prior to data collection. For data collection, crystals were briefly immerged in cryo buffer (100 mM imidazole pH 8.0, 34% (v/v) 2-methyl-2,4-pentanediol, supplemented with 20 mM compound 25) and shock-frozen in liquid nitrogen. A diffraction data set to 2.2 Å resolution was collected on beamline BL14.1 operated by Helmholtz-Zentrum Berlin (HZB) at the BESSY II electron storage ring (Berlin-Adlershof, Germany)(42) and processed using XDS(43) via the graphical user interface XDSAPP.(44) The structure was solved by rigid-body refinement with REFMAC5(45) with PDB entry 3HVL as starting model. The initial model was rebuilt in COOT(46) and refined using REFMAC5. Difference electron density indicated binding of the ligand only in one of the two PXR molecules of this crystal form (chain B). The ligand parameters were calculated using PRODRG,(47) and the ligand was fitted to the electron density using COOT. The remaining weak unexplained difference density suggested that a small fraction of the compound may also be bound in an alternative second conformation where the benzyl moiety may be flipped, but the density was too weak to justify, including this possible second conformation into the final model. Data collection and refinement statistics are summarized in Table 1 in the Supporting Information.

      P2X ion channel receptors and inflammation
      Burnstock, Geoffrey
      Purinergic Signalling (2016), 12 (1), 59-67CODEN: PSUIA9; ISSN:1573-9538. (Springer)
      Neuroinflammation limits tissue damage in response to pathogens or injury and promotes repair. There are two stages of inflammation, initiation and resoln. P2X receptors are gaining attention in relation to immunol. and inflammation. The P2X7 receptor in particular appears to be an essential immunomodulatory receptor, although P2X1 and P2X4 receptors also appear to be involved. ATP released from damaged or infected cells causes inflammation by release of inflammatory cytokines via P2X7 receptors and acts as a danger signal by occupying upregulated P2X receptors on immune cells to increase immune responses. The purinergic involvement in inflammation is being explored for the development of novel therapeutic strategies.

      2
      Purinergic mechanisms and pain
      Burnstock, Geoffrey
      Advances in Pharmacology (San Diego, CA, United States) (2016), 75 (Pharmacological Mechanisms and the Modulation of Pain), 91-137CODEN: ADPHEL; ISSN:1054-3589. (Elsevier Inc.)
      There is a brief introductory summary of purinergic signaling involving ATP storage, release, and ectoenzymic breakdown, and the current classification of receptor subtypes for purines and pyrimidines. The review then describes purinergic mechanosensory transduction involved in visceral, cutaneous, and musculoskeletal nociception and on the roles played by receptor subtypes in neuropathic and inflammatory pain. Multiple purinoceptor subtypes are involved in pain pathways both as an initiator and modulator. Activation of homomeric P2X3 receptors contributes to acute nociception and activation of heteromeric P2X2/3 receptors appears to modulate longer-lasting nociceptive sensitivity assocd. with nerve injury or chronic inflammation. In neuropathic pain activation of P2X4, P2X7, and P2Y12 receptors on microglia may serve to maintain nociceptive sensitivity through complex neural-glial cell interactions and antagonists to these receptors reduce neuropathic pain. Potential therapeutic approaches involving purinergic mechanisms will be discussed.

      Bo, X.; Burnstock, G. Triphosphate, the key structure of the ATP molecule responsible for interaction with P2X-purinoceptors. Gen. Pharmacol. 1993, 24, 637– 640,  DOI: 10.1016/0306-3623(93)90223-K
      [Crossref], [PubMed], [CAS], Google Scholar
      3
      Triphosphate, the key structure of the ATP molecule responsible for interaction with P2X-purinoceptors
      Bo, Xuenong; Burnstock, Goeffrey
      General Pharmacology (1993), 24 (3), 637-40CODEN: GEPHDP; ISSN:0306-3623.
      A radioligand binding assay was carried out to explore the key structure in mols. of ATP and its analogs responsible for the binding to P2X-purinoceptors. It was found that adenosine, adenine and xanthine had no effect on [3H]α,β-methylene ATP binding to membrane fractions prepd. from rat urinary bladder, while pentasodium triphosphate and disodium pyrophosphate could effectively displace the binding. Na orthophosphate was shown to displace the binding only at much higher concn. Apart from ATP, several other nucleotides could also fully displace the specific binding, but with potencies lower than that of ATP. The results indicate that the phosphate side chain in mols. of ATP and its analogs is the key structure responsible for the binding to P2X-purinoceptors.

      5
      Introduction and perspective, historical note
      Burnstock, Geoffrey
      Frontiers in Cellular Neuroscience (2013), 7 (Nov.), 227/1-227/13, 13 pp.CODEN: FCNRAH; ISSN:1662-5102. (Frontiers Media S.A.)
      A review. P2 nucleotide receptors were proposed to consist of two subfamilies based on pharmacol. in 1985, named P2X and P2Y receptors. Later, this was confirmed following cloning of the receptors for nucleotides and studies of transduction mechanisms in the early 1990s. P2X receptors are ion channels and seven subtypes are recognized that form trimeric homomultimers or heteromultimers. P2X receptors are involved in neuromuscular and synaptic neurotransmission and neuromodulation. They are also expressed on many types of non-neuronal cells to mediate smooth muscle contraction, secretion, and immune modulation. The emphasis in this review will be on the pathophysiol. of P2X receptors and therapeutic potential of P2X receptor agonists and antagonists for neurodegenerative and inflammatory disorders, visceral and neuropathic pain, irritable bowel syndrome, diabetes, kidney failure, bladder incontinence and cancer, as well as disorders if the special senses, airways, skin, cardiovascular, and musculoskeletal systems.

      P2 receptor mRNA expression profiles in human lymphocytes, monocytes and CD34+ stem and progenitor cells
      Wang Lingwei; Jacobsen Sten Eirik W; Bengtsson Anders; Erlinge David
      BMC immunology (2004), 5 (), 16 ISSN:.
      BACKGROUND: Extracellular nucleotides (ATP, ADP, UTP and UDP) exert a wide range of biological effects in blood cells mediated by multiple ionotropic P2X receptors and G protein-coupled P2Y receptors. Although pharmacological experiments have suggested the presence of several P2 receptor subtypes on monocytes and lymphocytes, some results are contradictory. Few physiological functions have been firmly established to a specific receptor subtype, partly because of a lack of truly selective agonists and antagonists. This stimulated us to investigate the expression of P2X and P2Y receptors in human lymphocytes and monocytes with a newly established quantitative mRNA assay for P2 receptors. In addition, we describe for the first time the expression of P2 receptors in CD34+ stem and progenitor cells implicating a potential role of P2 receptors in hematopoietic lineage and progenitor/stem cell function. RESULTS: Using a quantitative mRNA assay, we assessed the hypothesis that there are specific P2 receptor profiles in inflammatory cells. The P2X4 receptor had the highest expression in lymphocytes and monocytes. Among the P2Y receptors, P2Y12 and P2Y2 had highest expression in lymphocytes, while the P2Y2 and P2Y13 had highest expression in monocytes. Several P2 receptors were expressed (P2Y2, P2Y1, P2Y12, P2Y13, P2Y11, P2X1, P2X4) in CD34+ stem and progenitor cells. CONCLUSIONS: The most interesting findings were the high mRNA expression of P2Y12 receptors in lymphocytes potentially explaining the anti-inflammatory effects of clopidogrel, P2Y13 receptors in monocytes and a previously unrecognised expression of P2X4 in lymphocytes and monocytes. In addition, for the first time P2 receptor mRNA expression patterns was studied in CD34+ stem and progenitor cells. Several P2 receptors were expressed (P2Y2, P2Y1, P2Y12, P2Y13, P2Y11, P2X1, P2X4), indicating a role in differentiation and proliferation. Thus, it is possible that specific antibodies to P2 receptors could be used to identify progenitors for monocytes, lymphocytes and megakaryocytes.

      Up-regulation of P2X4 receptors in spinal microglia after peripheral nerve injury mediates BDNF release and neuropathic pain
      Ulmann, Lauriane; Hatcher, Jon P.; Hughes, Jane P.; Chaumont, Severine; Green, Paula J.; Conquet, Francois; Buell, Gary N.; Reeve, Alison J.; Chessell, Iain P.; Rassendren, Francois
      Journal of Neuroscience (2008), 28 (44), 11263-11268CODEN: JNRSDS; ISSN:0270-6474. (Society for Neuroscience)
      ATP is a known mediator of inflammatory and neuropathic pain. However, the mechanisms by which specific purinergic receptors contribute to chronic pain states are still poorly characterized. Here, we demonstrate that in response to peripheral nerve injury, P2X4 receptors (P2X4R) are expressed de novo by activated microglia in the spinal cord. Using in vitro and in vivo models, we provide direct evidence that P2X4R stimulation leads to the release of BDNF from activated microglia and, most likely phosphorylation of the NR1 subunit of NMDA receptors in dorsal horn neurons of the spinal cord. Furthermore, ATP stimulation is unable to stimulate BDNF release from P2X4-deficient mice microglia in primary cultures. These results indicate that P2X4R contribute to chronic pain through a central inflammatory pathway. P2X4R might thus represent a potential therapeutic target to limit microglia-mediated inflammatory responses assocd. with brain injury and neurodegenerative disorders.

      9
      P2X4: A fast and sensitive purinergic receptor
      Suurvali Jaanus; Boudinot Pierre; Kanellopoulos Jean; Ruutel Boudinot Sirje
      Biomedical journal (2017), 40 (5), 245-256 ISSN:.
      Extracellular nucleotides have been recognized as important mediators of activation, triggering multiple responses via plasma membrane receptors known as P2 receptors. P2 receptors comprise P2X ionotropic receptors and G protein-coupled P2Y receptors. P2X receptors are expressed in many tissues, where they are involved in a number of functions including synaptic transmission, muscle contraction, platelet aggregation, inflammation, macrophage activation, differentiation and proliferation, neuropathic and inflammatory pain. P2X4 is one of the most sensitive purinergic receptors (at nanomolar ATP concentrations), about one thousand times more than the archetypal P2X7. P2X4 is widely expressed in central and peripheral neurons, in microglia, and also found in various epithelial tissues and endothelial cells. It localizes on the plasma membrane, but also in intracellular compartments. P2X4 is preferentially localized in lysosomes, where it is protected from proteolysis by its glycosylation. High ATP concentration in the lysosomes does not activate P2X4 at low pH; P2X4 gets activated by intra-lysosomal ATP only in its fully dissociated tetra-anionic form, when the pH increases to 7.4. Thus, P2X4 is functioning as a Ca(2+)-channel after the fusion of late endosomes and lysosomes. P2X4 modulates major neurotransmitter systems and regulates alcohol-induced responses in microglia. P2X4 is one of the key receptors mediating neuropathic pain. However, injury-induced upregulation of P2X4 expression is gender dependent and plays a key role in pain difference between males and females. P2X4 is also involved in inflammation. Extracellular ATP being a pro-inflammatory molecule, P2X4 can trigger inflammation in response to high ATP release. It is therefore involved in multiple pathologies, like post-ischemic inflammation, rheumatoid arthritis, airways inflammation in asthma, neurodegenerative diseases and even metabolic syndrome. Although P2X4 remains poorly characterized, more studies are needed as it is likely to be a potential therapeutic target in these multiple pathologies.

      Meng, Xiao-wen; Gao, Jian-ling; Zuo, Jian-Ling; Wang, Li-Na; Liu, Si-lan; Jin, Xiao-Hong; Yao, Ming; Namaka, Michael

      Microglia in the spinal cord and neuropathic pain
      Tsuda, Makoto
      Journal of Diabetes Investigation (2016), 7 (1), 17-26CODEN: JDIOAY; ISSN:2040-1124. (Wiley-Blackwell)
      In contrast to physiol. pain, pathol. pain is not dependent on the presence of tissue-damaging stimuli. One type of pathol. pain - neuropathic pain - is often a consequence of nerve injury or of diseases such as diabetes. Neuropathic pain can be agonizing, can persist over long periods and is often resistant to known painkillers. A growing body of evidence shows that many pathol. processes within the central nervous system are mediated by complex interactions between neurons and glial cells. In the case of painful peripheral neuropathy, spinal microglia react and undergo a series of changes that directly influence the establishment of neuropathic pain states. After nerve damage, purinergic P2X4 receptors (non-selective cation channels activated by extracellular ATP) are upregulated in spinal microglia in a manner that depends on the transcription factors interferon regulatory factor 8 and 5, both of which are expressed in microglia after peripheral nerve injury. P2X4 receptor expression on the cell surface of microglia is also regulated at the post-translational level by signaling from CC chemokine receptor chemotactic cytokine receptor 2. Furthermore, spinal microglia in response to extracellular stimuli results in signal transduction through intracellular signaling cascades, such as mitogen-activated protein kinases, p38 and extracellular signal-regulated protein kinase. Importantly, inhibiting the function or expression of these microglial mols. suppresses the aberrant excitability of dorsal horn neurons and neuropathic pain. These findings show that spinal microglia are a central player in mechanisms for neuropathic pain, and might be a potential target for treating the chronic pain state.

      P2X4R+ microglia drive neuropathic pain
      Beggs, Simon; Trang, Tuan; Salter, Michael W.
      Nature Neuroscience (2012), 15 (8), 1068-1073CODEN: NANEFN; ISSN:1097-6256. (Nature Publishing Group)
      A review. Neuropathic pain, the most debilitating of all clin. pain syndromes, may be a consequence of trauma, infection or pathol. from diseases that affect peripheral nerves. Here we provide a framework for understanding the spinal mechanisms of neuropathic pain as distinct from those of acute pain or inflammatory pain. Recent work suggests that a specific microglia response phenotype characterized by de novo expression of the purinergic receptor P2X4 is crit. for the pathogenesis of pain hypersensitivity caused by injury to peripheral nerves. Stimulating P2X4 receptors initiates a core pain signaling pathway mediated by release of brain-derived neurotrophic factor, which produces a disinhibitory increase in intracellular chloride in nociceptive (pain-transmitting) neurons in the spinal dorsal horn. The changes caused by signaling from P2X4R+ microglia to nociceptive transmission neurons may account for the main symptoms of neuropathic pain in humans, and they point to specific interventions to alleviate this debilitating condition.

      P2rx4 deficiency in mice alleviates allergen-induced airway inflammation
      Zech Andreas; Wiesler Benjamin; Ayata Cemil Korcan; Schlaich Tilmann; Durk Thorsten; Hossfeld Madelon; Ehrat Nicolas; Cicko Sanja; Idzko Marco
      Oncotarget (2016), 7 (49), 80288-80297 ISSN:.
      Compelling evidences point out a crucial role for extracellular nucleotides such as adenosine triphosphate (ATP) during inflammatory conditions. Once released into the extracellular space, ATP modulates migration, maturation and function of various inflammatory cells via activating of purinergic receptors of the P2Y- and P2X- family. P2RX4 is an ATP-guided ion channel expressed on structural cells such as alveolar epithelial and smooth muscle cells as well as inflammatory cells including macrophages, dendritic cells (DCs) and T cells. P2RX4 has been shown to interact with P2RX7 and promote NLRP3 inflammasome activation. Although P2RX7 has already been implicated in allergic asthma, the role of P2RX4 in airway inflammation has not been elucidated yet. Furthermore, P2rx4-deficient bone marrow derived DCs (BMDCs) showed a reduced IL-1ss production in response to ATP accompanied by a decreased P2rx7 expression and attenuated Th2 priming capacity compared to wild type (WT) BMDCs in vitro. Moreover, mice adoptively transferred with P2rx4-deficient BMDCs exhibit a diminished AAI in vivo. In conclusion our data suggests that P2RX4-signaling contributes to AAI pathogenesis by regulating DC mediated Th2 cell priming via modulating IL-1ss secretion and selective P2RX4-antagonists might be a new therapeutic option for allergic asthma.

      Chen, Hongxia; Xia, Qingqing; Feng, Xiaoqian; Cao, Fangyuan; Yu, Hang; Song, Yinli; Ni, Xiuqin
      Molecular Medicine Reports (2016), 13 (1, Pt. B), 697-704CODEN: MMROA5; ISSN:1791-2997. (Spandidos Publications Ltd.)

      19
      Purinergic transmission in blood vessels
      Ralevic, Vera; Dunn, William R.
      Autonomic Neuroscience (2015), 191 (), 48-66CODEN: ANUEB2; ISSN:1566-0702. (Elsevier B.V.)
      A review. There are nineteen different receptor proteins for adenosine, adenine and uridine nucleotides, and nucleotide sugars, belonging to three families of G protein-coupled adenosine and P2Y receptors, and ionotropic P2X receptors. The majority are functionally expressed in blood vessels, as purinergic receptors in perivascular nerves, smooth muscle and endothelial cells, and roles in regulation of vascular contractility, immune function and growth have been identified. The endogenous ligands for purine receptors, ATP, ADP, UTP, UDP and adenosine, can be released from different cell types within the vasculature, as well as from circulating blood cells, including erythrocytes and platelets. Many purine receptors can be activated by two or more of the endogenous ligands. Further complexity arises because of interconversion between ligands, notably adenosine formation from the metab. of ATP, leading to complex integrated responses through activation of different subtypes of purine receptors. The enzymes responsible for this conversion, ectonucleotidases, are present on the surface of smooth muscle and endothelial cells, and may be coreleased with neurotransmitters from nerves. What selectivity there is for the actions of purines/pyrimidines comes from differential expression of their receptors within the vasculature. P2X1 receptors mediate the vasocontractile actions of ATP released as a neurotransmitter with noradrenaline (NA) from sympathetic perivascular nerves, and are located on the vascular smooth muscle adjacent to the nerve varicosities, the sites of neurotransmitter release. The relative contribution of ATP and NA as functional cotransmitters varies with species, type and size of blood vessel, neuronal firing pattern, the tone/pressure of the blood vessel, and in aging and disease. ATP is also a neurotransmitter in non-adrenergic non-cholinergic perivascular nerves and mediates vasorelaxation via smooth muscle P2Y-like receptors. ATP and adenosine can act as neuromodulators, with the most robust evidence being for prejunctional inhibition of neurotransmission via A1 adenosine receptors, but also prejunctional excitation and inhibition of neurotransmission via P2X and P2Y receptors, resp. P2Y2, P2Y4 and P2Y6 receptors expressed on the vascular smooth muscle are coupled to vasocontraction, and may have a role in pathophysiol. conditions, when purines are released from damaged cells, or when there is damage to the protective barrier that is the endothelium. Adenosine is released during hypoxia to increase blood flow via vasodilator A2A and A2B receptors expressed on the endothelium and smooth muscle. ATP is released from endothelial cells during hypoxia and shear stress and can act at P2Y and P2X4 receptors expressed on the endothelium to increase local blood flow. Activation of endothelial purine receptors leads to the release of nitric oxide, hyperpolarizing factors and prostacyclin, which inhibits platelet aggregation and thus ensures patent blood flow. Vascular purine receptors also regulate endothelial and smooth muscle growth, and inflammation, and thus are involved in the underlying processes of a no. of cardiovascular diseases.
      >> More from SciFinder ®

Purinergic P2X4 receptors and mitochondrial ATP production regulate T cell migration. J. Clin. Invest. 2018, 128, 3583– 3594,  DOI: 10.1172/JCI120972
